			<h1 lang="en" class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading">Apple</h1>
									<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
									<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div lang="en" class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the technology company, see <a title="Apple Inc." href="/wiki/Apple_Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> For other uses, see <a title="Apple (disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Apple_%28disambiguation%29">Apple (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>apple tree</b> (<i><a title="Malus" href="/wiki/Malus">Malus</a> pumila</i>, commonly and <a title="Error" href="/wiki/Error">erroneously</a> called <i>Malus domestica</i>) is a <a title="Deciduous" href="/wiki/Deciduous">deciduous</a> <a title="Tree" href="/wiki/Tree">tree</a> in the <a title="Rosaceae" href="/wiki/Rosaceae">rose family</a> best known for its sweet, <a title="Pome" href="/wiki/Pome">pomaceous</a> <a title="Fruit" href="/wiki/Fruit">fruit</a>, the <b>apple</b>. It is <a title="Agriculture" href="/wiki/Agriculture">cultivated</a> worldwide as a <a title="Fruit tree" href="/wiki/Fruit_tree">fruit tree</a>, and is the most widely grown species in the <a title="Genus" href="/wiki/Genus">genus</a> <i><a title="Malus" href="/wiki/Malus">Malus</a>.</i> The tree originated in <a title="Central Asia" href="/wiki/Central_Asia">Central Asia</a>, where its wild ancestor, <i><a title="Malus sieversii" href="/wiki/Malus_sieversii">Malus sieversii</a></i>, is still found today. Apples have been grown for thousands of years in Asia and Europe, and were brought to North America by <a title="European colonization of the Americas" href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas">European colonists</a>. Apples have <a title="Religion" href="/wiki/Religion">religious</a> and <a title="Mythology" href="/wiki/Mythology">mythological</a> significance in many cultures, including <a title="Norse mythology" href="/wiki/Norse_mythology">Norse</a>, <a title="Greek mythology" href="/wiki/Greek_mythology">Greek</a> and <a title="Christianity in Europe" href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Europe">European Christian traditions</a>.</p>
<p>Apple trees are large if grown from seed. Generally apple <a title="Cultivar" href="/wiki/Cultivar">cultivars</a> are propagated by <a title="Grafting" href="/wiki/Grafting">grafting</a> onto rootstocks, which control the size of the resulting tree. There are more than 7,500 known <a title="List of apple cultivars" href="/wiki/List_of_apple_cultivars">cultivars of apples</a>, resulting in a range of desired characteristics. Different cultivars are bred for various tastes and uses, including <a title="Cooking apple" href="/wiki/Cooking_apple">cooking</a>, eating raw and <a title="Apple cider" href="/wiki/Apple_cider">cider</a> production. Trees and fruit are prone to a number of <a title="Fungus" href="/wiki/Fungus">fungal</a>, <a title="Bacteria" href="/wiki/Bacteria">bacterial</a> and pest problems, which can be controlled by a number of <a title="Organic farming" href="/wiki/Organic_farming">organic</a> and non-organic means. In 2010, the fruit's <a title="Genome" href="/wiki/Genome">genome</a> was <a title="DNA sequencing" href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing">sequenced</a> as part of research on disease control and selective breeding in apple production.</p>
<p>Worldwide production of apples in 2014 was 84.6 million <a title="Tonnes" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes">tonnes</a>, with China accounting for 48% of the total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span id="Botanical_information" class="mw-headline">Botanical information</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg"><img width="220" height="276" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="465" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg/330px-Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg/440px-Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg/220px-Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malus_domestica_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-108.jpg"></a></div>
Blossoms, fruits, and leaves of the apple tree (<i>Malus pumila</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The apple is a <a title="Deciduous" href="/wiki/Deciduous">deciduous</a> tree, generally standing 1.8 to 4.6 m (6 to 15 ft) tall in cultivation and up to 12 m (39 ft) in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> When cultivated, the size, shape and branch density are determined by <a title="Rootstock" href="/wiki/Rootstock">rootstock</a> selection and trimming method. The <a title="Leaf" href="/wiki/Leaf">leaves</a> are <a title="Leaf arrangement" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaf_arrangement">alternately arranged</a> dark green-colored simple ovals with serrated margins and slightly downy undersides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><a title="Blossoms" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blossoms">Blossoms</a> are produced in spring simultaneously with the budding of the leaves, and are produced on spurs and some long shoots. The 3 to 4 cm (1.2 to 1.6 in) flowers are white with a pink tinge that gradually fades, five <a title="Petal" href="/wiki/Petal">petaled</a>, with an <a title="Inflorescence" href="/wiki/Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> consisting of a <a title="Cyme (botany)" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyme_%28botany%29">cyme</a> with 4–6 flowers. The central flower of the inflorescence is called the "king bloom"; it opens first, and can develop a larger fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fruit matures in late summer or autumn, and cultivars exist with a wide range of sizes. Commercial growers aim to produce an apple that is 7.0 to 8.3 cm (2.75 to 3.25 in) in diameter, due to market preference. Some consumers, especially those in Japan, prefer a larger apple, while apples below 5.7 cm (2.25 in) are generally used for making juice and have little fresh market value. The skin of ripe apples is generally red, yellow, green, pink, or russetted although many bi- or tri-colored cultivars may be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janick_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janick-7">[7]</a></sup> The skin may also be wholly or partly russeted i.e. rough and brown. The skin is covered in a protective layer of <a title="Epicuticular wax" href="/wiki/Epicuticular_wax">epicuticular wax</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The exocarp (flesh) is generally pale yellowish-white,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janick_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janick-7">[7]</a></sup> though pink or yellow exocarps also occur.</p>
<h3><span id="Wild_ancestors" class="mw-headline">Wild ancestors</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a title="Malus sieversii" href="/wiki/Malus_sieversii">Malus sieversii</a></div>
<p>The original wild <a title="Ancestor" href="/wiki/Ancestor">ancestor</a> of <i>Malus pumila</i> was <i>Malus sieversii</i>, found growing wild in the <a title="Mountains of Central Asia" href="/wiki/Mountains_of_Central_Asia">mountains of Central Asia</a> in southern <a title="Kazakhstan" href="/wiki/Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a title="Kyrgyzstan" href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, <a title="Tajikistan" href="/wiki/Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, and <a title="Xinjiang" href="/wiki/Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Architecture_and_size_relations:_an_essay_on_the_apple_.28Malus_x_domestica.2C_Rosaceae.29_tree_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Architecture_and_size_relations:_an_essay_on_the_apple_.28Malus_x_domestica.2C_Rosaceae.29_tree-9">[9]</a></sup> Cultivation of the species, most likely beginning on the forested flanks of the <a title="Tian Shan" href="/wiki/Tian_Shan">Tian Shan</a> mountains, progressed over a long period of time and permitted secondary <a title="Introgression" href="/wiki/Introgression">introgression</a> of genes from other species into the open-pollinated seeds. Significant exchange with <i><a title="Malus sylvestris" href="/wiki/Malus_sylvestris">Malus sylvestris</a></i>, the crabapple, resulted in current populations of apples being more related to crabapples than to the more morphologically similar progenitor <i>Malus sieversii</i>. In strains without recent admixture the contribution of the latter predominates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coart_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coart-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Genome" class="mw-headline">Genome</span></h3>
<p>In 2010, an Italian-led consortium announced they had sequenced the complete <a title="Genome" href="/wiki/Genome">genome</a> of the apple in collaboration with horticultural genomicists at <a title="Washington State University" href="/wiki/Washington_State_University">Washington State University</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkB_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkB-13">[13]</a></sup> using '<a title="Golden Delicious" href="/wiki/Golden_Delicious">Golden Delicious</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> It had about 57,000 genes, the highest number of any plant genome studied to date<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and more genes than the human genome (about 30,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This new understanding of the apple genome will help scientists in identifying genes and gene variants that contribute to resistance to disease and drought, and other desirable characteristics. Understanding the genes behind these characteristics will allow scientists to perform more knowledgeable selective breeding. The genome sequence also provided proof that <i>Malus sieversii</i> was the wild ancestor of the domestic apple—an issue that had been long-debated in the scientific community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkB_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkB-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="History" class="mw-headline">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:95apple.jpeg"><img width="220" height="165" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/95apple.jpeg/330px-95apple.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/95apple.jpeg/440px-95apple.jpeg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/95apple.jpeg/220px-95apple.jpeg" alt="color photograph of a hand holding a red apple" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:95apple.jpeg"></a></div>
Wild <i>Malus sieversii</i> apple in Kazakhstan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The center of diversity of the genus <i><a title="Malus" href="/wiki/Malus">Malus</a></i> is in eastern present-day <a title="Turkey" href="/wiki/Turkey">Turkey</a>. The apple tree was perhaps the earliest tree to be cultivated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app6_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-app6-17">[17]</a></sup> and its fruits have been improved through selection over thousands of years. <a title="Alexander the Great" href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great">Alexander the Great</a> is credited with finding dwarfed apples in <a title="Kazakhstan" href="/wiki/Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> in 328 <a title="BCE" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE">BCE</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup> those he brought back to Macedonia might have been the progenitors of dwarfing root stocks. Winter apples, picked in late autumn and stored just above freezing, have been an important food in Asia and Europe for millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app6_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-app6-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apples were introduced to North America by colonists in the 17th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup> and the first apple orchard on the North American continent was planted in <a title="Boston" href="/wiki/Boston">Boston</a> by Reverend <a title="William Blaxton" href="/wiki/William_Blaxton">William Blaxton</a> in 1625.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The only apples native to North America are <a title="Crab apples" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crab_apples">crab apples</a>, which were once called "common apples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawrence122_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-lawrence122-19">[19]</a></sup> Apple cultivars brought as seed from Europe were spread along Native American trade routes, as well as being cultivated on colonial farms. An 1845 United States apples nursery catalogue sold 350 of the "best" cultivars, showing the proliferation of new North American cultivars by the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawrence122_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-lawrence122-19">[19]</a></sup> In the 20th century, irrigation projects in <a title="Eastern Washington" href="/wiki/Eastern_Washington">Eastern Washington</a> began and allowed the development of the multibillion-dollar fruit industry, of which the apple is the leading product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until the 20th century, farmers stored apples in <a title="Root cellar" href="/wiki/Root_cellar">frostproof cellars</a> during the winter for their own use or for sale. Improved transportation of fresh apples by train and road replaced the necessity for storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In the 21st century, long-term storage again came into popularity, as "<a title="Controlled atmosphere" href="/wiki/Controlled_atmosphere">controlled atmosphere</a>" facilities were used to keep apples fresh year-round. Controlled atmosphere facilities use high humidity, low oxygen, and controlled carbon dioxide levels to maintain fruit freshness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Society_and_culture" class="mw-headline">Society and culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a title="Apple (symbolism)" href="/wiki/Apple_%28symbolism%29">Apple (symbolism)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:132px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg"><img width="130" height="183" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="390" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg/195px-Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg/260px-Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg/130px-Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg" alt="Illustration of girl in a red dress, holding 3 candles in one hand and a basket of apples in the other" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Larsson_Brita_as_Iduna.jpg"></a></div>
"Brita as <a title="Iðunn" href="/wiki/I%C3%B0unn">Iduna</a>" (1901) by <a title="Carl Larsson" href="/wiki/Carl_Larsson">Carl Larsson</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span id="Germanic_paganism" class="mw-headline">Germanic paganism</span></h3>
<p>In <a title="Norse mythology" href="/wiki/Norse_mythology">Norse mythology</a>, the goddess <a title="Iðunn" href="/wiki/I%C3%B0unn">Iðunn</a> is portrayed in the <i><a title="Prose Edda" href="/wiki/Prose_Edda">Prose Edda</a></i> (written in the 13th century by <a title="Snorri Sturluson" href="/wiki/Snorri_Sturluson">Snorri Sturluson</a>) as providing apples to the <a title="Gods" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gods">gods</a> that give them <a title="Eternal youth" href="/wiki/Eternal_youth">eternal youthfulness</a>. English scholar <a title="H. R. Ellis Davidson" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H._R._Ellis_Davidson">H. R. Ellis Davidson</a> links apples to religious practices in <a title="Germanic paganism" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism">Germanic paganism</a>, from which <a title="Norse paganism" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_paganism">Norse paganism</a> developed. She points out that buckets of apples were found in the <a title="Oseberg ship" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oseberg_ship">Oseberg ship</a> burial site in Norway, and that fruit and nuts (Iðunn having been described as being transformed into a nut in <i><a title="Skáldskaparmál" href="/wiki/Sk%C3%A1ldskaparm%C3%A1l">Skáldskaparmál</a></i>) have been found in the early graves of the <a title="Germanic peoples" href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples">Germanic peoples</a> in England and elsewhere on the continent of Europe, which may have had a symbolic meaning, and that nuts are still a recognized symbol of <a title="Fertility" href="/wiki/Fertility">fertility</a> in southwest England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Davidson notes a connection between apples and the <a title="Vanir" href="/wiki/Vanir">Vanir</a>, a tribe of gods associated with <a title="Fertility" href="/wiki/Fertility">fertility</a> in Norse mythology, citing an instance of eleven "golden apples" being given to woo the beautiful <a title="Gerðr" href="/wiki/Ger%C3%B0r">Gerðr</a> by <a title="Skírnir" href="/wiki/Sk%C3%ADrnir">Skírnir</a>, who was acting as messenger for the major Vanir god <a title="Freyr" href="/wiki/Freyr">Freyr</a> in stanzas 19 and 20 of <i><a title="Skírnismál" href="/wiki/Sk%C3%ADrnism%C3%A1l">Skírnismál</a></i>. Davidson also notes a further connection between fertility and apples in Norse mythology in chapter 2 of the <i><a title="Völsunga saga" href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lsunga_saga">Völsunga saga</a></i> when the major goddess <a title="Frigg" href="/wiki/Frigg">Frigg</a> sends King <a title="Rerir" href="/wiki/Rerir">Rerir</a> an apple after he prays to Odin for a child, Frigg's messenger (in the guise of a crow) drops the apple in his lap as he sits atop a <a title="Tumulus" href="/wiki/Tumulus">mound</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSON165-166_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSON165-166-25">[25]</a></sup> Rerir's wife's consumption of the apple results in a six-year pregnancy and the <a title="Caesarean section" href="/wiki/Caesarean_section">Caesarean section</a> birth of their son—the hero <a title="Völsung" href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lsung">Völsung</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONROLES146-147_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONROLES146-147-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further, Davidson points out the "strange" phrase "Apples of <a title="Hel (location)" href="/wiki/Hel_%28location%29">Hel</a>" used in an 11th-century poem by the <a title="Skald" href="/wiki/Skald">skald</a> Thorbiorn Brúnarson. She states this may imply that the apple was thought of by Brúnarson as the food of the dead. Further, Davidson notes that the potentially Germanic goddess <a title="Nehalennia" href="/wiki/Nehalennia">Nehalennia</a> is sometimes depicted with apples and that parallels exist in early Irish stories. Davidson asserts that while cultivation of the apple in Northern Europe extends back to at least the time of the <a title="Roman Empire" href="/wiki/Roman_Empire">Roman Empire</a> and came to Europe from the <a title="Near East" href="/wiki/Near_East">Near East</a>, the native varieties of apple trees growing in Northern Europe are small and bitter. Davidson concludes that in the figure of Iðunn "we must have a dim reflection of an old symbol: that of the guardian goddess of the life-giving fruit of the other world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Greek_mythology" class="mw-headline">Greek mythology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:132px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg"><img width="130" height="228" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="564" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg/195px-Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg/260px-Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg/130px-Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hercules_Musei_Capitolini_MC1265_n2.jpg"></a></div>
<a title="Heracles" href="/wiki/Heracles">Heracles</a> with the apple of <a title="Hesperides" href="/wiki/Hesperides">Hesperides</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apples appear in many <a title="World religions" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_religions">religious traditions</a>, often as a mystical or <a title="Forbidden fruit" href="/wiki/Forbidden_fruit">forbidden fruit</a>. One of the problems identifying apples in religion, <a title="Mythology" href="/wiki/Mythology">mythology</a> and <a title="Folklore" href="/wiki/Folklore">folktales</a> is that the word "apple" was used as a generic term for all (foreign) fruit, other than berries, including nuts, as late as the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-app3-27">[27]</a></sup> For instance, in <a title="Greek mythology" href="/wiki/Greek_mythology">Greek mythology</a>, the <a title="Greek hero" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_hero">Greek hero</a> <a title="Heracles" href="/wiki/Heracles">Heracles</a>, as a part of his <a title="Labours of Hercules" href="/wiki/Labours_of_Hercules">Twelve Labours</a>, was required to travel to the Garden of the Hesperides and pick the golden apples off the <a title="Hesperides" href="/wiki/Hesperides#The_Garden_of_the_Hesperides">Tree of Life</a> growing at its center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-book1-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-book2-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Greek goddess of discord, <a title="Eris (mythology)" href="/wiki/Eris_%28mythology%29">Eris</a>, became disgruntled after she was excluded from the wedding of <a title="Peleus" href="/wiki/Peleus">Peleus</a> and <a title="Thetis" href="/wiki/Thetis">Thetis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In retaliation, she tossed a golden apple inscribed <a title="Kallisti" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kallisti">Καλλίστη</a> (<i>Kalliste</i>, sometimes transliterated <i>Kallisti</i>, 'For the most beautiful one'), into the wedding party. Three goddesses claimed the apple: <a title="Hera" href="/wiki/Hera">Hera</a>, <a title="Athena" href="/wiki/Athena">Athena</a>, and <a title="Aphrodite" href="/wiki/Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>. <a title="Paris (mythology)" href="/wiki/Paris_%28mythology%29">Paris</a> of <a title="Troy" href="/wiki/Troy">Troy</a> was appointed to select the recipient. After being bribed by both Hera and Athena, Aphrodite tempted him with the most beautiful woman in the world, <a title="Helen of Troy" href="/wiki/Helen_of_Troy">Helen</a> of <a title="Sparta" href="/wiki/Sparta">Sparta</a>. He awarded the apple to Aphrodite, thus indirectly causing the <a title="Trojan War" href="/wiki/Trojan_War">Trojan War</a>.</p>
<p>The apple was thus considered, in ancient Greece, to be sacred to Aphrodite, and to throw an apple at someone was to symbolically declare one's love; and similarly, to catch it was to symbolically show one's acceptance of that love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> An epigram claiming authorship by Plato states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I throw the apple at you, and if you are willing to love me, take it and share your girlhood with me; but if your thoughts are what I pray they are not, even then take it, and consider how short-lived is beauty.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a title="Plato" href="/wiki/Plato">Plato</a>, Epigram VII<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a title="Atalanta" href="/wiki/Atalanta">Atalanta</a>, also of Greek mythology, raced all her suitors in an attempt to avoid marriage. She outran all but <a title="Hippomenes" href="/wiki/Hippomenes">Hippomenes</a> (also known as <a title="Melanion" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melanion">Melanion</a>, a name possibly derived from <i>melon</i> the Greek word for both "apple" and fruit in general),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book2_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-book2-29">[29]</a></sup> who defeated her by cunning, not speed. Hippomenes knew that he could not win in a fair race, so he used three golden apples (gifts of Aphrodite, the goddess of love) to distract Atalanta. It took all three apples and all of his speed, but Hippomenes was finally successful, winning the race and Atalanta's hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book1_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-book1-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Christian_art" class="mw-headline">Christian art</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg"><img width="220" height="276" data-file-height="2934" data-file-width="2337" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg/330px-Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg/440px-Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg/220px-Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29_2.jpg"></a></div>
<i><a title="Adam and Eve" href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve">Adam and Eve</a></i> by <a title="Albrecht Dürer" href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer">Albrecht Dürer</a> (1507), showcasing the apple as a symbol of sin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though the forbidden fruit of <a title="Garden of Eden" href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden">Eden</a> in the <a title="Book of Genesis" href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis">Book of Genesis</a> is not identified, popular Christian tradition has held that it was an apple that <a title="Adam and Eve" href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve">Eve</a> coaxed <a title="Adam and Eve" href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve">Adam</a> to share with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book7_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-book7-34">[34]</a></sup> The origin of the popular identification with a fruit unknown in the Middle East in biblical times is found in confusion between the <a title="Latin language" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language">Latin</a> words <i>mālum</i> (an apple) and <i>mălum</i> (an evil), each of which is normally written <i>malum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The tree of the forbidden fruit is called "the tree of the knowledge of good and evil" in <a href="http://www.esvbible.org/Genesis+2:17" class="external text" rel="nofollow">Genesis 2:17</a>, and the Latin for "good and evil" is <i>bonum et malum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p><a title="Renaissance" href="/wiki/Renaissance">Renaissance</a> painters may also have been influenced by the story of the <a title="Golden apple" href="/wiki/Golden_apple">golden apples</a> in the <a title="Hesperides" href="/wiki/Hesperides#The_Garden_of_the_Hesperides">Garden of Hesperides</a>. As a result, in the story of Adam and Eve, the apple became a symbol for knowledge, immortality, temptation, the fall of man into sin, and sin itself. The <a title="Larynx" href="/wiki/Larynx">larynx</a> in the human throat has been called the "<a title="Adam's apple" href="/wiki/Adam%27s_apple">Adam's apple</a>" because of a notion that it was caused by the forbidden fruit remaining in the throat of Adam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book7_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-book7-34">[34]</a></sup> The apple as symbol of sexual <a title="Seduction" href="/wiki/Seduction">seduction</a> has been used to imply human sexuality, possibly in an ironic vein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book7_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-book7-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Cultivars" class="mw-headline">Cultivars</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a title="List of apple cultivars" href="/wiki/List_of_apple_cultivars">List of apple cultivars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg"><img width="220" height="165" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg/330px-Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg/440px-Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg/220px-Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_and_Green_apples_in_India.jpg"></a></div>
Red and green apples in India</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are more than 7,500 known <a title="Cultivar" href="/wiki/Cultivar">cultivars</a> of apples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elzebroek_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elzebroek-37">[37]</a></sup> Cultivars vary in their <a title="Crop yield" href="/wiki/Crop_yield">yield</a> and the ultimate size of the tree, even when grown on the same <a title="Rootstock" href="/wiki/Rootstock">rootstock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-England_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-England-38">[38]</a></sup> Different cultivars are available for <a title="Temperate" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate">temperate</a> and <a title="Subtropical" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical">subtropical</a> climates. The UK's National Fruit Collection, which is the responsibility of the Department of Environment Food and Rural Affairs, includes a collection of over 2,000 cultivars of apple tree in Kent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The University of Reading, which is responsible for developing the UK national collection database, provides access to search the national collection. The University of Reading's work is part of the European Cooperative Programme for Plant Genetic Resources of which there are 38 countries participating in the Malus/Pyrus work group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UK's national fruit collection database contains a wealth of information on the characteristics and origin of many apples, including alternative names for what is essentially the same 'genetic' apple cultivar. Most of these cultivars are bred for eating fresh (dessert apples), though some are cultivated specifically for cooking (<a title="Cooking apple" href="/wiki/Cooking_apple">cooking apples</a>) or producing <a title="Cider" href="/wiki/Cider">cider</a>. <a title="Cider apple" href="/wiki/Cider_apple">Cider apples</a> are typically too tart and astringent to eat fresh, but they give the beverage a rich flavor that dessert apples cannot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apples1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-apples1-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commercially popular apple cultivars are soft but crisp. Other desired qualities in modern commercial apple breeding are a colorful skin, absence of <a title="Russet apple" href="/wiki/Russet_apple">russeting</a>, ease of shipping, lengthy storage ability, high yields, disease resistance, common apple shape, and developed flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-England_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-England-38">[38]</a></sup> Modern apples are generally sweeter than older cultivars, as popular tastes in apples have varied over time. Most North Americans and Europeans favor sweet, subacid apples, but tart apples have a strong minority following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-World-42">[42]</a></sup> Extremely sweet apples with barely any acid flavor are popular in Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-World-42">[42]</a></sup> and especially <a title="Indian Subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Subcontinent">Indian Subcontinent</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apples1_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-apples1-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Old cultivars are often oddly shaped, russeted, and have a variety of textures and colors. Some find them to have a better flavor than modern cultivars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crops_.26_Gardening_-_Apples_of_Antiquity_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crops_.26_Gardening_-_Apples_of_Antiquity-43">[43]</a></sup> but they may have other problems which make them commercially unviable—low yield, disease susceptibility, poor tolerance for storage or transport, or just being the 'wrong' size. A few old cultivars are still produced on a large scale, but many have been preserved by home gardeners and farmers that sell directly to local markets. Many unusual and locally important cultivars with their own unique taste and appearance exist; apple conservation campaigns have sprung up around the world to preserve such local cultivars from extinction. In the United Kingdom, old cultivars such as '<a title="Cox's Orange Pippin" href="/wiki/Cox%27s_Orange_Pippin">Cox's Orange Pippin</a>' and '<a title="Egremont Russet" href="/wiki/Egremont_Russet">Egremont Russet</a>' are still commercially important even though by modern standards they are low yielding and susceptible to disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-app-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Cultivation" class="mw-headline">Cultivation</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Breeding" class="mw-headline">Breeding</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a title="Fruit tree propagation" href="/wiki/Fruit_tree_propagation">Fruit tree propagation</a> and <a title="Malling series" href="/wiki/Malling_series">Malling series</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg"><img width="220" height="147" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1792" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg/330px-Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg/440px-Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg/220px-Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apfelbaum_Winterrambour_Hochstamm.jpg"></a></div>
An apple tree in Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blooming_apple_tree.jpg"><img width="220" height="330" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="1536" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Blooming_apple_tree.jpg/330px-Blooming_apple_tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Blooming_apple_tree.jpg/440px-Blooming_apple_tree.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Blooming_apple_tree.jpg/220px-Blooming_apple_tree.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blooming_apple_tree.jpg"></a></div>
Blooming apple tree on <a title="Kota Batu, Indonesia" href="/wiki/Kota_Batu,_Indonesia">Kota Batu, Indonesia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the wild, apples grow readily from seeds. However, like most perennial fruits, apples are ordinarily propagated asexually by <a title="Grafting" href="/wiki/Grafting">grafting</a>. This is because seedling apples are an example of "<a title="Zygosity" href="/wiki/Zygosity">extreme heterozygotes</a>", in that rather than inheriting DNA from their parents to create a new apple with those characteristics, they are instead significantly different from their parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a title="Polyploid" href="/wiki/Polyploid">Triploid</a> cultivars have an additional reproductive barrier in that 3 sets of chromosomes cannot be divided evenly during meiosis, yielding unequal segregation of the chromosomes (aneuploids). Even in the case when a triploid plant can produce a seed (apples are an example), it occurs infrequently, and seedlings rarely survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because apples do not breed true when planted as seeds, <a title="Grafting" href="/wiki/Grafting">grafting</a> is generally used to produce new apple trees. The <a title="Rootstock" href="/wiki/Rootstock">rootstock</a> used for the bottom of the graft can be selected to produce trees of a large variety of sizes, as well as changing the winter hardiness, insect and disease resistance, and soil preference of the resulting tree. Dwarf rootstocks can be used to produce very small trees (less than 3.0 m (10 ft) high at maturity), which bear fruit earlier in their life cycle than full size trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Dwarf rootstocks for apple trees can be traced as far back as 300 BC, to the area of <a title="Persia" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia">Persia</a> and <a title="Asia Minor" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor">Asia Minor</a>. <a title="Alexander the Great" href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great">Alexander the Great</a> sent samples of dwarf apple trees to <a title="Aristotle" href="/wiki/Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <a title="Lyceum" href="/wiki/Lyceum">Lyceum</a>. Dwarf rootstocks became common by the 15th century, and later went through several cycles of popularity and decline throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The majority of the rootstocks used today to control size in apples were developed in England in the early 1900s. The <a title="East Malling Research Station" href="/wiki/East_Malling_Research_Station">East Malling Research Station</a> conducted extensive research into rootstocks, and today their rootstocks are given an "M" prefix to designate their origin. Rootstocks marked with an "MM" prefix are Malling-series cultivars later crossed with trees of '<a title="Northern Spy" href="/wiki/Northern_Spy">Northern Spy</a>' in <a title="London Borough of Merton" href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Merton">Merton, England</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most new apple cultivars originate as seedlings, which either arise by chance or are bred by deliberately crossing cultivars with promising characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book9_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-book9-49">[49]</a></sup> The words 'seedling', 'pippin', and 'kernel' in the name of an apple cultivar suggest that it originated as a seedling. Apples can also form <a title="Bud sport" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bud_sport">bud sports</a> (mutations on a single branch). Some bud sports turn out to be improved strains of the parent cultivar. Some differ sufficiently from the parent tree to be considered new cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app4_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-app4-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 1930s, the Excelsior Experiment Station at the <a title="University of Minnesota" href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a> has introduced a steady progression of important apples that are widely grown, both commercially and by local orchardists, throughout <a title="Minnesota" href="/wiki/Minnesota">Minnesota</a> and <a title="Wisconsin" href="/wiki/Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>. Its most important contributions have included '<a title="Haralson (apple)" href="/wiki/Haralson_%28apple%29">Haralson'</a> (which is the most widely cultivated apple in Minnesota), '<a title="Wealthy (apple)" href="/wiki/Wealthy_%28apple%29">Wealthy</a>', 'Honeygold', and '<a title="Honeycrisp" href="/wiki/Honeycrisp">Honeycrisp</a>'.</p>
<p>Apples have been acclimatized in Ecuador at very high altitudes, where they can often, with the needed factors, provide crops twice per year because of constant temperate conditions year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apples_in_Ecuador_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apples_in_Ecuador-51">[51]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span id="Pollination" class="mw-headline">Pollination</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a title="Fruit tree pollination" href="/wiki/Fruit_tree_pollination">Fruit tree pollination</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apple_tree_blossom.JPG"><img width="220" height="165" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Apple_tree_blossom.JPG/330px-Apple_tree_blossom.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Apple_tree_blossom.JPG/440px-Apple_tree_blossom.JPG 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Apple_tree_blossom.JPG/220px-Apple_tree_blossom.JPG" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apple_tree_blossom.JPG"></a></div>
Apple blossom from an old <a title="Ayrshire" href="/wiki/Ayrshire">Ayrshire</a> cultivar</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orchmason.jpg"><img width="220" height="190" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="474" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Orchmason.jpg/330px-Orchmason.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Orchmason.jpg/440px-Orchmason.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Orchmason.jpg/220px-Orchmason.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orchmason.jpg"></a></div>
<a title="Orchard mason bee" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orchard_mason_bee">Orchard mason bee</a> on apple bloom, <a title="British Columbia, Canada" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Columbia,_Canada">British Columbia, Canada</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apples are self-incompatible; they must <a title="Pollination" href="/wiki/Pollination">cross-pollinate</a> to develop fruit. During the flowering each season, apple growers often utilize <a title="Pollinator" href="/wiki/Pollinator">pollinators</a> to carry pollen. <a title="Honey bee" href="/wiki/Honey_bee">Honey bees</a> are most commonly used. <a title="Osmia lignaria" href="/wiki/Osmia_lignaria">Orchard mason bees</a> are also used as supplemental pollinators in commercial orchards. <a title="Bumblebee" href="/wiki/Bumblebee">Bumblebee</a> <a title="Queen bee" href="/wiki/Queen_bee">queens</a> are sometimes present in orchards, but not usually in enough quantity to be significant pollinators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app4_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-app4-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are four to seven pollination groups in apples, depending on climate:</p>
<ul>
<li>Group A – Early flowering, 1 to 3 May in England (<a title="Gravenstein" href="/wiki/Gravenstein">Gravenstein</a>, Red Astrachan)</li>
<li>Group B – 4 to 7 May (<a title="Idared" href="/wiki/Idared">Idared</a>, <a title="McIntosh (apple)" href="/wiki/McIntosh_%28apple%29">McIntosh</a>)</li>
<li>Group C – Mid-season flowering, 8 to 11 May (<a title="Granny Smith" href="/wiki/Granny_Smith">Granny Smith</a>, <a title="Cox's Orange Pippin" href="/wiki/Cox%27s_Orange_Pippin">Cox's Orange Pippin</a>)</li>
<li>Group D – Mid/late season flowering, 12 to 15 May (<a title="Golden Delicious" href="/wiki/Golden_Delicious">Golden Delicious</a>, Calville blanc d'hiver)</li>
<li>Group E – Late flowering, 16 to 18 May (<a title="Braeburn" href="/wiki/Braeburn">Braeburn</a>, Reinette d'Orléans)</li>
<li>Group F – 19 to 23 May (Suntan)</li>
<li>Group H – 24 to 28 May (Court-Pendu Gris - also called Court-Pendu plat)</li>
</ul>
<p>One cultivar can be pollinated by a compatible cultivar from the same group or close (A with A, or A with B, but not A with C or D).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Symposium_on_Growth_Regulators_in_Fruit_Production_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Symposium_on_Growth_Regulators_in_Fruit_Production-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cultivars are sometimes classified by the day of peak bloom in the average 30-day blossom period, with pollenizers selected from cultivars within a 6-day overlap period.</p>
<h3><span id="Maturation_and_harvest" class="mw-headline">Maturation and harvest</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a title="Fruit picking" href="/wiki/Fruit_picking">Fruit picking</a> and <a title="Fruit tree pruning" href="/wiki/Fruit_tree_pruning">Fruit tree pruning</a></div>
<p>Cultivars vary in their yield and the ultimate size of the tree, even when grown on the same rootstock. Some cultivars, if left unpruned, will grow very large, which allows them to bear much more fruit, but makes harvesting very difficult. Depending on the tree density (number of trees planted per unit surface area), mature trees typically bear 40–200 kg (88–441 lb) of apples each year, though productivity can be close to zero in poor years. Apples are harvested using three-point ladders that are designed to fit amongst the branches. Trees grafted on dwarfing rootstocks will bear about 10–80 kg (22–176 lb) of fruit per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app4_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-app4-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Crops ripen at different times of the year according to the cultivar. Cultivar that yield their crop in the summer include 'Gala', 'Golden Supreme', 'McIntosh', 'Transparent', 'Primate', '<a title="Sweet Bough" href="/wiki/Sweet_Bough">Sweet Bough</a>', and 'Duchess'; fall producers include 'Fuji', 'Jonagold', 'Golden Delicious', 'Red Delicious', 'Chenango', 'Gravenstein', 'Wealthy', 'McIntosh', 'Snow', and 'Blenheim'; winter producers include 'Winesap', 'Granny Smith', 'King', 'Wagener', '<a title="Swazie (apple)" href="/wiki/Swazie_%28apple%29">Swayzie</a>', 'Greening', and 'Tolman Sweet'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawrence122_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-lawrence122-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Storage" class="mw-headline">Storage</span></h3>
<p>Commercially, apples can be stored for some months in <a title="Controlled atmosphere" href="/wiki/Controlled_atmosphere">controlled atmosphere</a> chambers to delay <a title="Ethylene" href="/wiki/Ethylene">ethylene</a>-induced ripening. Apples are commonly stored in chambers with higher concentrations of <a title="Carbon dioxide" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and high air filtration. This prevents ethylene concentrations from rising to higher amounts and preventing ripening from occurring too quickly. Ripening continues when the fruit is removed from storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> For home storage, most cultivars of apple can be held for approximately two weeks when kept at the coolest part of the refrigerator (i.e. below 5 °C). Some, including '<a title="Granny Smith" href="/wiki/Granny_Smith">Granny Smith</a>' and '<a title="Fuji (apple)" href="/wiki/Fuji_%28apple%29">Fuji</a>', can be stored up to a year without significant degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Food_Science_Australia_Fact_Sheet:_Refrigerated_storage_of_perishable_foods_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Food_Science_Australia_Fact_Sheet:_Refrigerated_storage_of_perishable_foods-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yepsen_1994_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yepsen_1994-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-organic apples may be sprayed with <a title="1-methylcyclopropene" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1-methylcyclopropene">1-methylcyclopropene</a> blocking the apples’ ethylene receptors, temporarily preventing them from ripening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Pests_and_diseases" class="mw-headline">Pests and diseases</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg"><img width="220" height="165" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg/330px-Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg/440px-Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg/220px-Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apple_tree_leaves_with_insect_damage.jpg"></a></div>
Leaves with significant insect damage</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a title="List of apple diseases" href="/wiki/List_of_apple_diseases">List of apple diseases</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a title="List of Lepidoptera that feed on Malus" href="/wiki/List_of_Lepidoptera_that_feed_on_Malus">List of Lepidoptera that feed on Malus</a></div>
<p>Apple trees are susceptible to a number of <a title="Fungus" href="/wiki/Fungus">fungal</a> and <a title="Bacterium" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium">bacterial</a> diseases and insect pests. Many commercial orchards pursue a program of chemical sprays to maintain high fruit quality, tree health, and high yields. A trend in orchard management is the use of organic methods.<sup style="white-space:nowrap;" class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact">[<i><a title="Wikipedia:Citation needed" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These prohibit the use of synthetic pesticides, though some older pesticides are allowed. <a title="Organic farming" href="/wiki/Organic_farming">Organic</a> methods include, for instance, introducing its natural predator to reduce the population of a particular pest.</p>
<p>A wide range of pests and diseases can affect the plant; three of the more common diseases/pests are mildew, aphids and apple scab.</p>
<ul>
<li><a title="Mildew" href="/wiki/Mildew">Mildew</a>: which is characterized by light grey powdery patches appearing on the leaves, shoots and flowers, normally in spring. The flowers will turn a creamy yellow color and will not develop correctly. This can be treated in a manner not dissimilar from treating <a title="Botryotinia" href="/wiki/Botryotinia">Botrytis</a>; eliminating the conditions which caused the disease in the first place and burning the infected plants are among the recommended actions to take.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest4_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-pest4-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li><a title="Aphids" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphids">Aphids</a>: There are five species of aphids commonly found on apples: apple grain aphid, rosy apple aphid, apple aphid, spirea aphid and the woolly apple aphid. The aphid species can be identified by their color, the time of year when they are present and by differences in the cornicles, which are small paired projections from the rear of aphids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest4_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-pest4-58">[58]</a></sup> Aphids feed on foliage using needle-like mouth parts to suck out plant juices. When present in high numbers, certain species reduce tree growth and vigor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest5_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pest5-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li><a title="Apple scab" href="/wiki/Apple_scab">Apple scab</a>: Apple scab causes leaves to develop olive-brown spots with a velvety texture that later turn brown and become cork-like in texture. The disease also affects the fruit, which also develops similar brown spots with velvety or cork-like textures. Apple scab is spread through fungus growing in old apple leaves on the ground and spreads during warm spring weather to infect the new year's growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Among the most serious disease problems are <a title="Fireblight" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireblight">fireblight</a>, a bacterial disease; and <i><a title="Gymnosporangium" href="/wiki/Gymnosporangium">Gymnosporangium</a></i> rust, and <a title="Black Spot (disease)" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Spot_%28disease%29">black spot</a>, two fungal diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest5_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-pest5-59">[59]</a></sup> <a title="Codling moth" href="/wiki/Codling_moth">Codling moths</a> and <a title="Apple maggot" href="/wiki/Apple_maggot">apple maggots</a> are two other pests which affect apple trees. Young apple trees are also prone to mammal pests like mice and deer, which feed on the soft bark of the trees, especially in winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-60">[60]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span id="Production" class="mw-headline">Production</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a title="List of countries by apple production" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_apple_production">List of countries by apple production</a></div>
<p>World production of apples in 2014 was 84.6 million <a title="Tonne" href="/wiki/Tonne">tonnes</a>, with China producing 48% of this total (table).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-3">[3]</a></sup> Other major producers with 6% or less of the world total were the United States, Turkey, Poland and Italy.</p>

<p>A typical apple serving weighs 242 grams and provides 126 <a title="Calories" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories">calories</a> with a moderate content of <a title="Dietary fiber" href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber">dietary fiber</a> (table).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Otherwise, there is generally low content of <a title="Essential nutrients" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrients">essential nutrients</a> (table).</p>
<h2><span id="Human_consumption" class="mw-headline">Human consumption</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a title="Cooking apple" href="/wiki/Cooking_apple">Cooking apple</a> and <a title="Cider apple" href="/wiki/Cider_apple">Cider apple</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:172px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apple_stark_s.jpg"><img width="170" height="191" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="800" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Apple_stark_s.jpg/255px-Apple_stark_s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Apple_stark_s.jpg/340px-Apple_stark_s.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Apple_stark_s.jpg/170px-Apple_stark_s.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apple_stark_s.jpg"></a></div>
An apple core, the remainder of an apple that has been mostly eaten</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apples are often eaten raw. The whole fruit including the skin is suitable for human consumption except for the seeds, which may affect some consumers.<sup style="white-space:nowrap;" class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact">[<i><a title="Wikipedia:Citation needed" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The core is often not eaten and is discarded. Cultivars bred for raw consumption are termed dessert or <a title="Table apple" href="/wiki/Table_apple">table apples</a>.</p>
<p>Apples can be canned or juiced. They are milled or pressed to produce <a title="Apple juice" href="/wiki/Apple_juice">apple juice</a>, which may be drunk unfiltered (called <a title="Apple cider" href="/wiki/Apple_cider">apple cider</a> in North America), or filtered. The juice can be <a title="Fermentation (food)" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_%28food%29">fermented</a> to make <a title="Cider" href="/wiki/Cider">cider</a> (called hard cider in North America), <a title="Ciderkin" href="/wiki/Ciderkin">ciderkin</a>, and <a title="Vinegar" href="/wiki/Vinegar">vinegar</a>. Through <a title="Distillation" href="/wiki/Distillation">distillation</a>, various alcoholic beverages can be produced, such as <a title="Applejack (beverage)" href="/wiki/Applejack_%28beverage%29">applejack</a>, <a title="Calvados (spirit)" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvados_%28spirit%29">Calvados</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-food_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-food-62">[62]</a></sup> and <a title="Apfelwein" href="/wiki/Apfelwein">apfelwein</a>. <a title="Apple seed oil" href="/wiki/Apple_seed_oil">Apple seed oil</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and <a title="Pectin" href="/wiki/Pectin">pectin</a> may also be produced.</p>
<h3><span id="Popular_uses" class="mw-headline">Popular uses</span></h3>
<p>Apples are an important ingredient in many desserts, such as <a title="Apple pie" href="/wiki/Apple_pie">apple pie</a>, apple <a title="Crumble" href="/wiki/Crumble">crumble</a>, <a title="Apple crisp" href="/wiki/Apple_crisp">apple crisp</a> and <a title="Apple cake" href="/wiki/Apple_cake">apple cake</a>. They are often eaten <a title="Baked" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baked">baked</a> or <a title="Stewed" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stewed">stewed</a>, and they can also be dried and eaten or reconstituted (soaked in water, alcohol or some other liquid) for later use. When cooked, some apple cultivars easily form a puree known as <a title="Apple sauce" href="/wiki/Apple_sauce">apple sauce</a>. Apples are also made into <a title="Apple butter" href="/wiki/Apple_butter">apple butter</a> and apple jelly. They are also used (cooked) in meat dishes.</p>
<ul>
<li>In the UK, a <a title="Toffee apple" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toffee_apple">toffee apple</a> is a traditional confection made by coating an apple in hot <a title="Toffee" href="/wiki/Toffee">toffee</a> and allowing it to cool. Similar treats in the U.S. are <a title="Candy apple" href="/wiki/Candy_apple">candy apples</a> (coated in a hard shell of crystallized sugar syrup), and <a title="Caramel apples" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caramel_apples">caramel apples</a>, coated with cooled <a title="Caramel" href="/wiki/Caramel">caramel</a>.</li>
<li>Apples are eaten with honey at the Jewish New Year of <a title="Rosh Hashanah" href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah">Rosh Hashanah</a> to symbolize a sweet new year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-food_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-food-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>Farms with apple orchards may open them to the public, so consumers may themselves pick the apples they will purchase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-food_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-food-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Sliced apples turn brown with exposure to air due to the conversion of natural phenolic substances into <a title="Melanin" href="/wiki/Melanin">melanin</a> upon exposure to <a title="Oxygen" href="/wiki/Oxygen">oxygen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-J1-64">[64]</a></sup> Different cultivars vary in their propensity to brown after slicing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and the <a title="Genetically engineered" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_engineered">genetically engineered</a> <a title="Arctic Apples" href="/wiki/Arctic_Apples">Arctic Apples</a> do not brown. Sliced fruit can be treated with <a title="Acidulated water" href="/wiki/Acidulated_water">acidulated water</a> to prevent this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-J1-64">[64]</a></sup> Sliced apple consumption tripled in the US from 2004 to 2014 to 500 million apples annually due to its convenience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Organic_production" class="mw-headline">Organic production</span></h3>
<p><a title="Organic farming" href="/wiki/Organic_farming">Organic</a> apples are commonly produced in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organic_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-organic-67">[67]</a></sup> Due to infestations by key insects and diseases, organic production is difficult in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-croplife_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-croplife-68">[68]</a></sup> The use of pesticides containing chemicals, such as sulfur, copper, microorganisms, viruses, clay powders, or plant extracts (<a title="Pyrethrum" href="/wiki/Pyrethrum">pyrethrum</a>, <a title="Neem" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neem">neem</a>) has been approved by the EU Organic Standing Committee to improve organic yield and quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-croplife_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-croplife-68">[68]</a></sup> A light coating of <a title="Kaolin" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaolin">kaolin</a>, which forms a physical barrier to some pests, also may help prevent apple sun scalding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-app4_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-app4-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Phytochemicals" class="mw-headline">Phytochemicals</span></h3>
<p>Apples are a rich source of various <a title="Phytochemical" href="/wiki/Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a> including <a title="Flavonoid" href="/wiki/Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> (e.g., <a title="Catechin" href="/wiki/Catechin">catechins</a>, <a title="Flavanol" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavanol">flavanols</a>, and <a title="Quercetin" href="/wiki/Quercetin">quercetin</a>) and other <a title="Polyphenol" href="/wiki/Polyphenol">phenolic compounds</a> (e.g., <a title="Epicatechin" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicatechin">epicatechin</a> and <a title="Procyanidin" href="/wiki/Procyanidin">procyanidins</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribeiro2014_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro2014-69">[69]</a></sup> found in the skin, core, and pulp of the apple;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribeiro2014_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro2014-69">[69]</a></sup> they have unknown health value in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J1_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-J1-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p><a title="Ideain" href="/wiki/Ideain">Ideain</a> (cyanidin 3-O-galactoside) is an <a title="Anthocyanin" href="/wiki/Anthocyanin">anthocyanin</a>, a type of pigment, which is found in some red apple cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a title="Phlorizin" href="/wiki/Phlorizin">Phlorizin</a> is a flavonoid that is found in apple trees, particularly in the leaves, and in only small amounts if at all in other plants, even other species of the <i>Malus</i> genus or related plants such as pear trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Health_effects" class="mw-headline">Health effects</span></h3>
<p>Preliminary research is investigating whether nutrients and/or phytochemicals in apples may be preventive against the risk of some types of <a title="Cancer" href="/wiki/Cancer">cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribeiro2014_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro2014-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Allergy" class="mw-headline">Allergy</span></h3>
<p>One form of apple allergy, often found in northern Europe, is called birch-apple syndrome, and is found in people who are also allergic to <a title="Birch" href="/wiki/Birch">birch</a> <a title="Pollen" href="/wiki/Pollen">pollen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup> Allergic reactions are triggered by a protein in apples that is similar to birch pollen, and people affected by this protein can also develop allergies to other fruits, nuts, and vegetables. Reactions, which entail <a title="Oral allergy syndrome" href="/wiki/Oral_allergy_syndrome">oral allergy syndrome</a> (OAS), generally involve itching and inflammation of the mouth and throat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup> but in rare cases can also include life-threatening <a title="Anaphylaxis" href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> This reaction only occurs when raw fruit is consumed—the allergen is neutralized in the cooking process. The variety of apple, maturity and storage conditions can change the amount of allergen present in individual fruits. Long storage times can increase the amount of proteins that cause birch-apple syndrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="width:222px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg"><img width="220" height="147" data-file-height="1420" data-file-width="2130" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg/330px-MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg/440px-MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg/220px-MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_pommes.jpg"></a></div>
Different kinds of apple <a title="List of apple cultivars" href="/wiki/List_of_apple_cultivars">cultivars</a> in a wholesale food market</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In other areas, such as the Mediterranean, some individuals have adverse reactions to apples because of their similarity to peaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup> This form of apple allergy also includes OAS, but often has more severe symptoms, such as vomiting, abdominal pain and <a title="Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria">urticaria</a>, and can be life-threatening. Individuals with this form of allergy can also develop reactions to other fruits and nuts. Cooking does not break down the protein causing this particular reaction, so affected individuals can eat neither raw nor cooked apples. Freshly harvested, over-ripe fruits tend to have the highest levels of the protein that causes this reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breeding efforts have yet to produce a <a title="Hypoallergenic" href="/wiki/Hypoallergenic">hypoallergenic</a> fruit suitable for either of the two forms of apple allergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-EU-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Toxicity_of_seeds" class="mw-headline">Toxicity of seeds</span></h3>
<p>The seeds of apples contain small amounts of <a title="Amygdalin" href="/wiki/Amygdalin">amygdalin</a>, a sugar and <a title="Cyanide" href="/wiki/Cyanide">cyanide</a> compound known as a <a title="Cyanogenic glycoside" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanogenic_glycoside#Cyanogenic_glycosides">cyanogenic glycoside</a>. Ingesting small amounts of apple seeds will cause no ill effects, but consumption of extremely large doses can cause <a title="Adverse reaction" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_reaction">adverse reactions</a>. It may take several hours before the poison takes effect, as cyanogenic glycosides must be <a title="Hydrolyzed" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrolyzed">hydrolyzed</a> before the cyanide ion is released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NelsonShih2007_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NelsonShih2007-75">[75]</a></sup> The United States <a title="National Library of Medicine" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Medicine">National Library of Medicine</a>'s <a title="Hazardous Substances Data Bank" href="/wiki/Hazardous_Substances_Data_Bank">Hazardous Substances Data Bank</a> <a title="Case report" href="/wiki/Case_report">records no cases</a> of amygdalin poisoning from consuming apple seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Proverbs" class="mw-headline">Proverbs</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div style="width:290px;" class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg"><img width="288" height="98" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="2800" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg/432px-Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg/576px-Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg 2x" class="thumbimage" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg/288px-Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg" alt="" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a title="Enlarge" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pink_lady_and_cross_section.jpg"></a></div>
An apple's side, stem end, and interior</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a title="Proverb" href="/wiki/Proverb">proverb</a> "<i><a title="An apple a day keeps the doctor away" href="/wiki/An_apple_a_day_keeps_the_doctor_away">An apple a day keeps the doctor away</a></i>", addressing the health effects of the fruit, dates from 19th century Wales, according to Caroline Taggart, author of “An Apple a Day: Old-Fashioned Proverbs and Why They Still Work”. The original phrase, Taggart said, was: "Eat an apple on going to bed, and you’ll keep the doctor from earning his bread". In the 19th century and early 20th, the phrase evolved to “an apple a day, no doctor to pay” and “an apple a days sends the doctor away”, while the phrasing now commonly used was first recorded in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>

Votes: 6–11–1 (not recommended)", <i>Taxon</i>, <b>63</b> (6): 1358–1371, <a title="Digital object identifier" href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier">doi</a>:<a href="//doi.org/10.12705%2F636.20" class="external text" rel="nofollow">10.12705/636.20</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AApple&amp;rft.atitle=Report+of+the+Nomenclature+Committee+for+Vascular+Plants%3A+66%3A+%281933%29.+To+conserve+Malus+domestica+Borkh.+against+M.+pumila+Miller%2C+M.+communis+Desf.%2C+M.+frutescens+Medik.%2C+and+Pyrus+dioica+Moench+%28Rosaceae%29.+Proposed+by+G.-Z.+Qian%2C+L.-F.+Liu+%26+G.-G.+Tang+in+Taxon+59%282%29%3A+650%E2%80%93652.+2010.+Votes%3A+6%E2%80%9311%E2%80%931+%28not+recommended%29&amp;rft.au=Wendy+L.+Applequist&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=6&amp;rft.jtitle=Taxon&amp;rft.pages=1358-1371&amp;rft.volume=63&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.12705%2F636.20&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<td style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px" class="navbox-list navbox-odd">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">

<li><a title="List of apple diseases" href="/wiki/List_of_apple_diseases">Apple diseases</a></li>
